<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Kalancho&#235; genome provides insights into convergent evolution and building blocks of crassulacean acid metabolism"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-12-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) is a water-use efficient adaptation of photosynthesis that has evolved independently many times in diverse lineages of flowering plants. We hypothesize that convergent evolution of protein sequence and temporal gene expression underpins the independent emergences of CAM from C3 photosynthesis. To test this hypothesis, we generate a de novo genome assembly and genome-wide transcript expression data for Kalancho&#235; fedtschenkoi, an obligate CAM species within the core eudicots with a relatively small genome (~260&#8201;Mb). Our comparative analyses identify signatures of convergence in protein sequence and re-scheduling of diel transcript expression of genes involved in nocturnal CO2 fixation, stomatal movement, heat tolerance, circadian clock, and carbohydrate metabolism in K. fedtschenkoi and other CAM species in comparison with non-CAM species. These findings provide new insights into molecular convergence and building blocks of CAM and will facilitate CAM-into-C3 photosynthesis engineering to enhance water-use efficiency in crops. Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) is a metabolic adaptation of photosynthesis that enhances water use efficiency. Here, via genomic analysis of Kalancho&#235;, the authors provide evidence for convergent evolution of protein sequence and temporal gene expression underpinning the multiple independent emergences of CAM."/>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Nature Communications"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The Kalanchoë genome provides insights into convergent evolution and building blocks of crassulacean acid metabolism"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) is a metabolic adaptation of photosynthesis that enhances water use efficiency. Here, via genomic analysis of Kalanchoë, the authors provide evidence for convergent evolution of protein sequence and temporal gene expression underpinning the multiple independent emergences of CAM."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/m685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig1_HTML.jpg"/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) is a water-use efficient adaptation of photosynthesis that has evolved independently many times in diverse lineages of flowering plants. We hypothesize that convergent evolution of protein sequence and temporal gene expression underpins the independent emergences of CAM from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis. To test this hypothesis, we generate a de novo genome assembly and genome-wide transcript expression data for <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>, an obligate CAM species within the core eudicots with a relatively small genome (~260 Mb). Our comparative analyses identify signatures of convergence in protein sequence and re-scheduling of diel transcript expression of genes involved in nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation, stomatal movement, heat tolerance, circadian clock, and carbohydrate metabolism in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and other CAM species in comparison with non-CAM species. These findings provide new insights into molecular convergence and building blocks of CAM and will facilitate CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis engineering to enhance water-use efficiency in crops.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) is a metabolic adaptation of photosynthetic CO<sub>2</sub> fixation that enhances plant water-use efficiency (WUE) and associated drought avoidance/tolerance by reducing transpirational water loss through stomatal closure during the day, when temperatures are high, and stomatal opening during the night, when temperatures are lower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="West-Eberhard, M., Smith, J. &amp; Winter, K. Photosynthesis, reorganized. Science &#xA;                           332, 311–312 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1248">1</a></sup>. In the face of the rapidly increasing human population and global warming predicted over the next century, the outstanding WUE of CAM plants highlights the potential of the CAM pathway for sustainable food and biomass production on semi-arid, abandoned, or marginal agricultural lands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Borland, A. M., Griffiths, H., Hartwell, J. &amp; Smith, J. A. C. Exploiting the potential of plants with crassulacean acid metabolism for bioenergy production on marginal lands. J. Exp. Bot. &#xA;                           60, 2879–2896 (2009)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1252">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cushman, J. C., Davis, S. C., Yang, X. &amp; Borland, A. M. Development and use of bioenergy feedstocks for semi-arid and arid lands. J. Exp. Bot. &#xA;                           66, 4177–4193 (2015)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1252_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1255">4</a></sup>.</p><p>CAM photosynthesis can be divided into two major phases: (1) nocturnal uptake of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> through open stomata and primary fixation of CO<sub>2</sub> by phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC) to oxaloacetate (OAA) and its subsequent conversion to malic acid by malate dehydrogenase; and (2) daytime decarboxylation of malate and CO<sub>2</sub> refixation via C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis, mediated by ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase (RuBisCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Owen, N. A. &amp; Griffiths, H. A system dynamics model integrating physiology and biochemical regulation predicts extent of crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) phases. New Phytol. &#xA;                           200, 1116–1131 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1270">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1273">6</a></sup>. Malic acid is stored in the vacuole of photosynthetically active cells reaching a peak at dawn and can be used as a reference point to divide the two phases. CAM is found in over 400 genera across 36 families of vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1278">4</a></sup> and is thought to have evolved multiple times independently from diverse ancestral C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Silvera, K. et al. Evolution along the crassulacan acid metabolism continuum. Funct. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           37, 995–1010 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1284">7</a></sup>. The core biochemical characteristics of the CAM cycle are similar in all the plant lineages in which CAM has evolved, with some variation in the enzymes that catalyze malate decarboxylation during the day, and in the storage carbohydrates that provide substrates for malic acid synthesis at night<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Christopher, J. &amp; Holtum, J. Patterns of carbohydrate partitioning in the leaves of crassulacean acid metabolism species during deacidification. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           112, 393–399 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1288">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Holtum, J. A. M., Smith, J. A. C. &amp; Neuhaus, H. E. Intracellular transport and pathways of carbon flow in plants with crassulacean acid metabolism. Funct. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           32, 429–449 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1291">9</a></sup>.</p><p>We hypothesize that convergent evolution in protein sequence and/or temporal diel gene expression underpins the multiple and independent emergences of CAM from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis. Convergent evolution is generally defined as the appearance of similar phenotypes in distinct evolutionary lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Washburn, J. D., Bird, K. A., Conant, G. C., Pires, J. C. &amp; Herendeen, P. S. Convergent evolution and the origin of complex phenotypes in the age of systems biology. Int. J. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           177, 305–318 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1300">10</a></sup>. Although phenotypic convergence is widely recognized, its evolutionary mechanism has been extensively debated. Morris<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Morris S. C. Life’s Solution: Inevitable Humans In A Lonely Universe. Cambridge University (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1304">11</a></sup> argues that the evolutionary course is not random but selection-constrained, along certain pathways, to arrive at the same solution or outcome. Recently, comparative genomics analysis began to provide new insight into the molecular mechanism of convergent evolution. For example, Foote et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Foote, A. D. et al. Convergent evolution of the genomes of marine mammals. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           47, 272–275 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1308">12</a></sup> performed comparative genomic analyses of three species of marine mammals (the killer whale, walrus, and manatee) that share independently evolved phenotypic adaptations to a marine existence, and identified convergent amino-acid substitutions in genes evolving under positive selection and putatively associated with a marine phenotype. Also, Hu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hu, Y. B. et al. Comparative genomics reveals convergent evolution between the bamboo-eating giant and red pandas. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           114, 1081–1086 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1312">13</a></sup> compared the genomes of the bamboo-eating giant and red pandas, two obligate bamboo-feeders that independently possess adaptive pseudothumbs, and identified 70 adaptively convergent genes (i.e., under positive selection in these two species), of which nine genes, featuring nonrandom convergent amino-acid substitution between giant and red pandas, are closely related to limb development and essential nutrient utilization. These two examples indicate that specific amino-acid replacements at a small number of key sites can result in highly predictable convergent outcomes, supporting the constrained selection theory of Morris<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Morris S. C. Life’s Solution: Inevitable Humans In A Lonely Universe. Cambridge University (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1317">11</a></sup>. However, such predictable protein sequence convergence was not found in the convergence of hemoglobin function in high-altitude-dwelling birds, indicating that possible adaptive solutions are perhaps contingent upon prior evolutionary history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Natarajan, C. et al. Predictable convergence in hemoglobin function has unpredictable molecular underpinnings. Science &#xA;                           354, 336–339 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1321">14</a></sup>. This finding supports the contingent adaptation theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gould S. J. Wonderful Life: The Burgess Shale And The Nature Of Life. Norton (1989)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1325">15</a></sup> that evolution is contingent upon history and consequently replaying life’s tape will give different outcomes. In addition to protein sequence convergence, convergent changes in gene expression were found to be associated with convergent evolution of vocal learning in the brains of humans and song-learning birds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Pfenning, A. R. et al. Convergent transcriptional specializations in the brains of humans and song-learning birds. Science &#xA;                           346, 1256846 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1329">16</a></sup>. Therefore, convergent changes in both protein sequence and gene expression are important aspects of the molecular basis of convergent evolution.</p><p>We sought to investigate whether changes in protein sequence and/or gene expression contribute to the evolutionary convergence of CAM through genome-wide screening for signatures of convergent changes in protein sequences and diel mRNA expression patterns that meet the following criteria: the signatures are (1) isomorphic in the CAM genomes of distant groups, such as eudicots and monocots, which diverged ~135 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Magallón, S., Gómez-Acevedo, S., Sánchez-Reyes, L. L. &amp; Hernández-Hernández, T. A metacalibrated time-tree documents the early rise of flowering plant phylogenetic diversity. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 437–453 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1336">17</a></sup>, and (2) dimorphic in related C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis genomes. Recently, the genome sequences of two monocot CAM species, <i>Ananas comosus</i> (L.) Merr. (pineapple)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1345">18</a></sup>, and <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> (Schauer) Rchb.f. (moth orchid)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1353">19</a></sup>, were published. Here we present the genome sequence of <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i> Raym.-Hamet &amp; H. Perrier, which is an emerging molecular genetic model for obligate CAM species in the eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1360">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1363">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hartwell, J., Dever, L. V. &amp; Boxall, S. F. Emerging model systems for functional genomics analysis of crassulacean acid metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           31, 100–108 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1366">20</a></sup>. Our analyses reveal the genomic signatures of convergence shared between eudicot (represented by <i>Kalanchoë</i>) and monocot (represented by pineapple and orchid) CAM species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">
                           <i>Kalanchoë</i> genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The diploid <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> (2n = 2x = 34 chromosomes; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">1</a>) genome size was estimated to be ~260 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">1</a>). The <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome was assembled from ~70× paired-end reads and ~37× mate-pair reads generated using an Illumina MiSeq platform (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">2</a>). The genome assembly consisted of 1324 scaffolds with a total length of 256 Mb and scaffold N50 of 2.45 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">3</a>), in which we predicted and annotated 30,964 protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">The phylogenetic placement of <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                        </h3><p>
                           <i>Kalanchoë</i> is the first eudicot CAM lineage with a genome sequence to date and serves as an important reference for understanding the evolution of CAM. In addition, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> is the first sequenced species in the distinct eudicot lineage, Saxifragales. Although the monophyly of this morphologically diverse order is well supported by molecular data, its phylogenetic placement has been less clear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Soltis, D. E., Soltis, P. S., Endress, P. K., &amp; Chase, M. W. Phylogeny And Evolution Of Angiosperms. Sinauer Associates Inc. (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1432">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Phylogenetic relationships and character evolution analysis of Saxifragales using a supermatrix approach. Am. J. Bot. &#xA;                           100, 916–929 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1435">22</a></sup>. The recent consensus view, based mainly on analyses of plastid DNA sequences, has placed the Saxifragales as a sister group to the rosids, and together they comprise the large clade of superrosids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. &#xA;                           181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1439">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. &#xA;                           214, 1338–1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1442">24</a></sup>. However, there have been indications of conflict between trees based on plastid genomes and nuclear genomes for this clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1446">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. &#xA;                           214, 1338–1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1449">24</a></sup>. Additionally, the major lineages of core eudicots are thought to have diversified rapidly following their first appearance, making resolution of the relationships among these clades particularly challenging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Magallón, S., Gómez-Acevedo, S., Sánchez-Reyes, L. L. &amp; Hernández-Hernández, T. A metacalibrated time-tree documents the early rise of flowering plant phylogenetic diversity. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 437–453 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1454">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Moore, M. J., Soltis, P. S., Bell, C. D., Burleigh, J. G. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Phylogenetic analysis of 83 plastid genes further resolves the early diversification of eudicots. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           107, 4623–4628 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1457">25</a></sup> and implicating incomplete lineage sorting (ILS) as a potentially important process that would result in discordance among gene histories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Maddison, W. P. &amp; Knowles, L. L. Inferring phylogeny despite incomplete lineage sorting. Syst. Biol. &#xA;                           55, 21–30 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1461">26</a></sup>.</p><p>We performed phylogenetic analyses with 210 single-copy nuclear genes from 26 sequenced plant genomes using multiple phylogenetic inference strategies. The resulting species trees are congruent with each other except for the placement of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, which was placed either as sister to the rosids in a phylogenetic tree reconstructed using a quartet-based coalescent species tree method (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig1">1</a>) or as sister to all other core eudicots as revealed by alternative phylogenetic trees reconstructed from (1) concatenated protein sequence alignment without gene partition using maximum-likelihood (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">3</a>), (2) a partitioned analysis of multi-gene alignment using maximum-likelihood and Bayesian methods (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">4</a>), and (3) analysis of individual gene trees using fully Bayesian multispecies coalescent method (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>). Despite substantial discordance among estimated nuclear gene trees, the coalescence-based tree was consistent with the results of the plastome-based analyses, placing <i>Kalanchoë</i> as sister to the rosids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig1">1</a>). Coalescent species tree estimation can account for gene tree discordance due to ILS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Degnan, J. H. &amp; Rosenberg, N. A. Gene tree discordance, phylogenetic inference and the multispecies coalescent. Trends Ecol. Evol. &#xA;                           24, 332–340 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1490">27</a></sup>. At the same time, alternative placements of <i>Kalanchoë</i> as sister to the asterids, or as sister to all other core eudicots were observed in many gene trees (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>). Gene tree discordance due to rapid diversification early in eudicot history has also been characterized by others<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. &#xA;                           214, 1338–1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1504">24</a></sup>. Regardless of the optimal placement of the Saxifragales, including <i>Kalanchoë</i>, individual gene trees will often have alternative histories due to ILS in the face of rapid species diversification.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>A species tree reconstructed from 210 single-copy genes using a summary method. <b>a</b> Diploid plant of <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>b</b> Individual maximum-likelihood gene trees were reconstructed from the CDS alignments for each of the 210 single-copy-gene ortholog groups using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1530">78</a></sup>, and the species tree was summarized from the gene trees using ASTRAL-II<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, i44–i52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1534">79</a></sup>. Pie graphs on nodes represent the proportion of gene trees that support the various quartets at every node, with red for the main topology shown in this tree, blue for the first alternative, and green for the second alternative, respectively. Quartet frequencies displayed in pie graphs and the posterior-probability at each node are calculated by ASTRAL-II<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, i44–i52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1539">79</a></sup>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">
                           <i>Kalanchoë</i> genome duplication</h3><p>The grape genome has no additional genome duplication after the ancestral gamma hexaploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Murat, F. et al. Karyotype and gene order evolution from reconstructed extinct ancestors highlight contrasts in genome plasticity of modern rosid crops. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           7, 735–749 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1564">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature &#xA;                           449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1567">29</a></sup> and is the best available reference for studying ancestral eudicot genome duplication events. Syntenic depth analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature &#xA;                           492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1571">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Amborella Genome Project.. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science &#xA;                           342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1574">31</a></sup> showed that there are multiple <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> blocks covering each grape gene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">6</a>). Specifically, 65% of the grape genome had from one to four syntenic blocks in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. In contrast, a sudden drop in syntenic depth occurred after a depth of 4× (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig2">2a</a>), indicating that each grape genome region has up to four <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> blocks and thus providing strong evidence for two distinct whole-genome duplications (WGDs) events in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. The microsynteny patterns further support two WGDs on the lineages leading to <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. Specifically, the microsynteny pattern reflects a 1:4 gene copy ratio between the grape genome and the diploid <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Genome duplication in <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>a</b> Syntenic depth of the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome for each grape gene. Syntenic depth refers to the number of times a genomic region is covered by synteny blocks against another genome. <b>b</b> Typical micro-colinearity patterns between genomic regions from grape and <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. Rectangles show predicted gene models with colors showing relative orientations (blue: same strand, black: opposite strand). Matching gene pairs are displayed as connecting shades. Three orthologous gene groups that were maximally retained as four copies in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> were highlighted with phylogenetic trees on the right suggesting two rounds of genome duplications in the <i>Kalanchoë</i> lineage. <b>c</b> Four-fold transversion substitution rate (4dtv) in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and six other eudicot plant species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>From the <i>Kalanchoë</i> point of view, we found that 49% of the <i>Kalanchoë</i> genome was covered by one grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> block, 7% covered in two grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> blocks, and 1% covered in three grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> blocks (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">7</a>). This suggests that we could often find one best grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> block out of the three gamma triplicated regions in grape. This fits the scenario that the gamma WGD predated the divergence and there has been no WGD in the grape lineage since grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> diverged. Alternatively, if the divergence predated the gamma WGD, then from the <i>Kalanchoë</i> point of view we should instead see three matching grape regions. Hence, the grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> genome comparisons strongly supported the gamma WGD as a shared event, and further supported the phylogenetic position of <i>Kalanchoë</i> in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig1">1</a>.</p><p>Despite two apparent WGDs in the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> lineage, synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) between duplicate gene pairs showed only one prominent peak ~0.35 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>). The unimodal distribution of Ks suggests the two WGD events occurring close in time. Similarly, two distinct peaks appear in the distribution of the four-fold transversion substitution rate (4dtv) values between the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig2">2c</a>). Grape-<i>Kalanchoë</i> gene pairs show a prominent peak around Ks = 1.5 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>), indicating that the WGDs in the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> lineage occurred well after its divergence from grape early in the history of the rosid lineage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene co-expression modules and clusters in <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                        </h3><p>To elucidate gene function in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, we performed a weighted correlation network analysis of transcript expression in 16 samples including 12 mature leaf samples collected every 2 h over a 24-h period and four non-leaf samples collected 4 h after the beginning of the light period, including shoot tip (leaf pair 1 plus the apical meristem), stem (between leaf pair 3 and leaf pair 8), root, and flower. Our analysis identified 25 co-expression modules, among which one module (MEblack containing 782 genes) was significantly (Student’s <i>t</i>-test, <i>P &lt; </i>0.001) associated with the leaf samples collected during the dark period (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">9</a>), with an increase in transcript abundance at night (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a>). Several biological processes (e.g., carboxylic acid biosynthesis, terpene biosynthesis, and lipid metabolism) were over-represented (hypergeometric enrichment test, <i>P &lt; </i>0.05) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM3">1</a>), and several key genes encoding proteins involved in nocturnal CAM carboxylation and vacuolar uptake of malate such as Kaladp0018s0289 (<i>β-CA</i>), Kaladp0048s0578 (<i>PEPC2</i>), Kaladp0037s0517 (<i>PPCK</i>), Kaladp0022s0111 (<i>MDH</i>), and Kaladp0062s0038 (<i>ALMT6</i>) were present in this module (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>). These results suggest that genes in the co-expression module MEblack play important roles in the nighttime processes that define CAM. One alternate module (MEblue containing 1911 genes) was significantly correlated with the leaf samples collected during the day (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">9</a>), with an increase in transcript abundance during the light period (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a>). Several biological processes (e.g., starch biosynthesis, coenzyme biosynthetic process) were over-represented (hypergeometric enrichment test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) in this module (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM3">1</a>). One gene in the CAM decarboxylation process, Kaladp0010s0106 (<i>PPDK-RP</i>), belongs to this module (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>An overview of CAM pathway in <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>a</b> The CAM pathway map in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. Orange colors indicate the key enzymes involved in the CAM pathway. The numbers in parenthesis are the four-fold transversion substitution rate (4dtv) values. <b>b</b> Diel expression profiles of duplicated genes in CAM-related gene families. ALMT tonoplast aluminum-activated malate transporter, β-CA β type carbonic anhydrase, ME malic enzyme, MDH malate dehydrogenase, PEP phosphoenolpyruvate, PEPC PEP carboxylase, PPCK PEPC kinase, PPDK pyruvate phosphate dikinase, TDT tonoplast dicarboxylate transporter. White and black bars indicate daytime (12-h) and nighttime (12-h), respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We also performed cluster analysis on the CAM leaf time-course expression data for the transcripts that showed significantly (ANOVA of glm models where H<sub>0</sub> = a flat line, <i>P &lt; </i>0.05) time-structured diel expression patterns as determined by a polynomial regression. Clustering of transcripts with time-structured expression identified 11 clusters (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">11</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">7</a>). Networks constructed for each cluster implicated highly connected hub genes and their direct or indirect interactions with CAM-related genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM4">2</a>). For example, cluster 7, which contains <i>PEPC1</i> (Kaladp0095s0055) and <i>PPCK2</i> (Kaladp0604s0001), has a zinc-finger protein <i>CONSTANS-like</i> gene as a central hub (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM4">2</a>). <i>CONSTANS-like</i> genes are part of the circadian clock regulatory network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ledger, S., Strayer, C., Ashton, F., Kay, S. A. &amp; Putterill, J. Analysis of the function of two circadian-regulated CONSTANS-LIKE genes. Plant J. &#xA;                           26, 15–22 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1865">32</a></sup>. Similarly, multiple <i>REVEILLE</i> transcripts, which encode transcription factors for genes with evening elements in their promoters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hsu, P. Y. &amp; Harmer, S. L. Wheels within wheels: the plant circadian system. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 240–249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d49345e1873">33</a></sup>, are hubs in cluster 4 that contains <i>NADP-ME</i> genes (Kaladp0092s0166) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Overview of genes that have undergone convergent evolution</h3><p>To determine the possibility that the diel reprogramming of metabolism that distinguishes CAM from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis was achieved, at least in part, by convergent shifts in diel patterns of gene expression, we performed comparative analysis of diel transcript abundance patterns in CAM and C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species. Specifically, we compared the diel expression patterns of 9733 ortholog groups of genes from <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> (eudicot, CAM photosynthesis), <i>A. comosus</i> (monocot, CAM photosynthesis), and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (eudicot, C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis), with transcript abundances &gt;0.01 FPKM in mature leaf samples collected at six or more diel time points. Sampling time points included dawn (22, 24, and 2 h from the start of the light period), midday (4, 6, and 8 h from the start of the light period), dusk (10, 12, and 14 h from the start of the light period), and midnight (16, 18, and 20 h from the start of the light period) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4a</a>). A gene from <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> was defined as having undergone convergent evolution of gene expression if it met all of the following criteria: (1) its diel transcript expression pattern was highly correlated (Spearman’s rank correlation coefficient, <i>r</i> &gt; 0.8) with those of at least one of the orthologs in <i>A. comosus</i>, but not highly correlated (<i>r</i> &lt; 0.5) with those of any of the orthologs in <i>A. thaliana</i>; (2) it displayed a significant difference (false discovery rate &lt;0.01) in transcript abundance either between midday and midnight (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4b</a>), or between dawn and dusk (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4</a>c); and (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig3">3</a>) the time shift between <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>A. comosus</i> transcript time-courses was less than or equal to 3 h, whereas the time shifts between CAM species (<i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>A. comosus</i>) transcripts and their <i>A. thaliana</i> ortholog transcript were equal to or greater than 6 h. Based on these criteria, 54 <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes were identified as candidates for involvement in the convergent shift in diel gene expression patterns specific to the two CAM species relative to <i>A. thaliana</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">2</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Examples of convergent change in diel transcript expression pattern in CAM species. <b>a</b> The four time-windows for comparative analysis of temporal changes in transcript expression, which were represented by 12 time points: 2, 4, …, 24 h after the beginning of the light period. <b>b</b> and <b>c</b> Comparison of diel transcript expression pattern of phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase kinase 1 (<i>PPCK1</i>) and phototropin 2 (<i>PHOT2</i>), respectively, between CAM species (<i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i> and pineapple) and C<sub>3</sub> species (<i>Arabidopsis</i>). Left panels show the diel transcript expression profiles. Right panels show enrichment triangle networks, in which a <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene and a pineapple ortholog had significantly enriched expression in the same time-window, whereas an <i>Arabidopsis</i> ortholog had significantly enriched expression in the opposite time-window. The numbers are the time shifts in diel transcript expression pattern between genes connected by each edge. White and black bars indicate daytime (12-h) and nighttime (12-h), respectively. X-axis represents the time after the beginning of the light period</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>To identify genes that had likely undergone convergent evolution in protein sequence in the CAM species, we reconstructed gene tribes based on protein sequences from the species listed in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">4</a>. We then created phylogenetic trees for the genes from all tribes that include at least one gene from each of the 13 studied species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">9</a>). A <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene was defined as having undergone convergent evolution in protein sequence if it met all of the following criteria: (1) the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene is clustered with gene(s) from at least one of the two monocot CAM species (<i>A. comosus</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>) in a phylogenetic clade containing no genes from C<sub>3</sub> or C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis species; (2) convergent amino-acid changes were detected between the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene with gene(s) from at least one of the two monocot CAM species; and (3) the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene shared at least one amino-acid mutation with its ortholog in at least one of the two monocot CAM species, as compared with C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis species. A total of four <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes showing convergent changes in protein sequences were identified (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">15</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a>).</p><p>We also performed genome-wide positive selection analysis in each of the three CAM species (i.e., <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, and <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>) in comparison with 21 non-CAM species (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">1</a>) and identified two genes that were under positive selection in the dicot CAM species <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and one of the monocot CAM species (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Convergent evolution of genes involved in CO<sub>2</sub> fixation</h3><p>PEPC is a key enzyme for nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation and PPCK is a pivotal protein kinase that regulates PEPC in response to the circadian clock in CAM plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2104">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2107">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Hartwell J. The Circadian Clock in CAM Plants. in Annual Plant Reviews: Endogenous Plant Rhythms (ed Hall A. J. W., McWatters H.). Blackwell Publishing (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2110">34</a></sup>. PPCK phosphorylates PEPC in the dark (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig5">5a</a>) and thereby reduces malate inhibition of PEPC activity, promoting nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> uptake<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Hartwell, J., Nimmo, G., Wilkins, M., Jenkins, G. &amp; Nimmo, H. Phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase kinase is a novel protein kinase is a novel protein kinase regulated at the level of gene expression. Plant J. &#xA;                           20, 333–342 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2119">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Taybi, T., Patil, S., Chollet, R. &amp; Cushman, J. A minimal Ser/Thr protein kinase circadianly regulates phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase activity in CAM-induced leaves of Mesembryanthemum crystallinum. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           123, 1471–1482 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2122">36</a></sup>. Multiple PPCK genes were identified in the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome, among which two genes (Kaladp0037s0517 and Kaladp0604s0001) showed higher transcript abundance than the others in CAM leaves (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>). The diel expression patterns of the most abundant PPCK transcripts in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> (Kaladp0037s0517.1) and <i>A. comosus</i> (Aco013938.1) were highly correlated, with only a 1.5-hour time shift between them, whereas both showed an ~11-hour time shift relative to their best matched ortholog in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (AT1G08650) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>). Peak PPCK transcript abundance was shifted from daytime in C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species (<i>Arabidopsis</i>) to nighttime in the two CAM species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4b</a>), which suggests convergence and is consistent with PPCK activation of PEPC-mediated nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation. Among the PEPC genes identified in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, Kaladp0095s0055 and Kaladp0048s0578 showed higher transcript abundance than the others (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>). Kaladp0095s0055 (named <i>PEPC1</i> herein) was an abundant transcript throughout both the light and the dark period, with its peak transcript level phased to dusk. The second most abundant PEPC transcript (Kaladp0048s0578, named <i>PEPC2</i> herein) showed a much higher transcript level during the dark period than during the light period (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig5">5b</a>). We found that a duplicated pair of <i>K. fedtschenkoi PEPC2</i> genes (Kaladp0048s0578 and Kaladp0011s0355) clustered together with a PEPC gene (PEQU_07008) from <i>P. equestris</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>). PEQU_07008 was recently reported as the CAM-type PEPC in <i>P. equestris</i>, and, like Kaladp0048s0578, this orchid <i>PEPC</i> gene also showed higher transcript abundance during the dark period than during the light period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, L. et al. Origin and mechanism of crassulacean acid metabolism in orchids as implied by comparative transcriptomics and genomics of the carbon fixation pathway. Plant J. &#xA;                           86, 175–185 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2191">37</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Two phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC) genes with relative high transcript abundance in <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>a</b> Regulation of PEPC1 activity. <b>b</b> Diel expression profiles of <i>PEPC1</i> (Kaladp0095s0055.1) and <i>PEPC2</i> (Kaladp0048s0578.1) transcripts in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, shown in the left and right <i>Y</i>-axis, respectively. OAA Oxaloacetate, PEP phospho<i>enol</i>pyruvate, PEPC PEP carboxylase, PPCK PEPC kinase, PP2A protein phosphatase 2 A. White and black bars indicate daytime (12-h) and nighttime (12-h), respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Convergent changes in PEPC2 protein sequence were found between <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig6">6a, b</a>). Specifically, multiple protein sequence alignment revealed that an aspartic acid residue (D509) in Kaladp0048s0578 is conserved in PEQU_07008 and Kaladp0011s0355 (a duplicated copy of Kaladp0048s0578), but there was an arginine (R), lysine (K), or histidine (H) in the corresponding sites of the PEPC protein sequences of other tested species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig6">6c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>). The structural model of the Kaladp0048s0578 protein indicates that this single amino-acid substitution (from a basic amino-acid R/K/H to an acidic amino-acid D) is located in an α-helix adjacent to the active site in a β-barrel (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig7">7a</a>). We hypothesize that an activator binds to the active site of one subunit of the tetrameric complex of PEPC2, leading to allosteric conformational changes that subsequently activate another subunit of the tetramer (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig7">7b</a>). This model was supported by a recent crystallography structure of the <i>Flavaria trinervia</i> (a C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis plant) PEPC with an activator glucose-6-phosphate (G6P) bound at the β-barrel active center<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schlieper, D., Förster, K., Paulus, J. K. &amp; Groth, G. Resolving the activation site of positive regulators in plant phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase. Mol. Plant &#xA;                           7, 437–440 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2269">38</a></sup>. Based on this model, because D509 of PEPC2 (Kaladp0048s0578) is also negatively charged as G6P, the observed substitution may play a similar role as the activator by triggering allosteric conformational changes that lead to activation of the other subunits of PEPC tetramer. Nimmo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Nimmo, H. G. The regulation of phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase in CAM plants. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           5, 75–80 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2273">39</a></sup> reported that PEPC is subject to posttranslational regulation in the dark via phosphorylation by PPCK. <i>In vitro</i> analysis of the activities of different heterologously expressed PEPC isoforms showed that without phosphorylation by PPCK, PEPC1 from <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> had a much lower activity than PEPC2 from either <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> or <i>P. equestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig6">6d</a>). Further, the R515D mutation significantly (Student’s <i>t</i>-test, <i>P &lt; </i>0.01) increased the activity of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> PEPC1, whereas the D509K and D504K mutations significantly (Student’s <i>t</i>-test, <i>P &lt; </i>0.01) reduced the activities of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> PEPC2 and <i>P. equestris</i> PEPC2, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig6">6d</a>). These results indicate that a single amino-acid mutation could significantly modify PEPC activity.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>A convergent change in phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC) protein sequences in CAM species. <b>a</b> convergent- vs. divergent-substitutions in PEPC2 protein sequences between species listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM1">9</a>. The arrow head indicates the comparison of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> vs. <i>P. equestris</i>. <b>b</b> Probability of convergent changes in PEPC2 protein sequence between <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and orchid. Red arrow indicates the protein sequence alignment site of convergent change (highlighted in red font at the alignment in panel c). <b>c</b> A convergent amino-acid change (from R/K/H to D) in PEPC2 shared by diverse species (highlighted in red font) at the alignment position indicated by the red arrow. <b>d</b> 
                                       <i>In vitro</i> activity of PEPC isoforms in the absence of phosphorylation by PPCK. KfPEPC1: Kaladp0095s0055; KfPEPC1<sup>R515D</sup>: KfPEPC1 with mutation at residue 515 from arginine (R) to aspartic acid (D); KfPEPC2: Kaladp0048s0578.1; KfPEPC2<sup>D509K</sup>: KfPEPC2 with mutation at residue 509 from D to lysine (K); PqPEPC2: <i>P. equestris</i> PEPC gene PEQU07008; PqPEPC2<sup>D504K</sup>: PqPEPC2 with mutation at residue 504 from D to K. “*” indicates significant difference between wild-type and mutant of PEPC1 or PEPC2 (Student’s <i>t-</i>test; <i>P &lt; </i>0.01). The error bars indicate standard deviation (SD) calculated from three replicates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Protein structure model of phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase 2 (PEPC2) in <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>a</b> PEPC2 (Kaladp0048s0578.1) structural model with a glucose-6-phosphate (G6P) substrate (orange spheres) bound at the β-barrel active site (yellow). D509 (red spheres) is located at an α-helix (red) in adjacent to the β-barrel and far from the hallmark serine residue (S8, green spheres) that is the phosphorylation target of PPCK1. <b>b</b> PEPC tetramer structure. The phosphorylation site (S8, green) is located at the interphase of the tetramer and D509 (spheres) is located at the peripheral of the tetramer. The β-barrel active site is shown in red, and no G6P activator may be required for activation of the PEPC activity following the competitive activating model of PEPC</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Our evolutionary analyses did not detect convergent evolution in either protein sequence or diel transcription patterns for the various decarboxylation genes that are expressed in <i>Kalanchoë</i> and <i>A. comosus</i>. In <i>Kalanchoë</i>, NAD(P)-ME genes were highly expressed, whereas the expression of the PEPCK gene was very low (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">18</a>), consistent with the known high extractable activities of NAD-ME and NADP-ME in CAM leaves of <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dever, L. V., Boxall, S. F., Kneřová, J. &amp; Hartwell, J. Transgenic perturbation of the decarboxylation phase of crassulacean acid metabolism alters physiology and metabolism but has only a small effect on growth. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           167, 44–59 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2429">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Dittrich, P. Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide-specific “malic” enzyme in Kalanchoë daigremontiana and other plants exhibiting crassulacean acid metabolism. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           57, 310–314 (1976)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2432">41</a></sup>.
