By contrast, in <i>A. comosus</i> the transcript abundance of PEPCK was much higher than that of malic enzyme (ME) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">18</a>), supporting the model that malate decarboxylation in <i>Kalanchoë</i> is mediated by ME, which was recently substantiated using a transgenic RNAi approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hartwell, J., Dever, L. V. &amp; Boxall, S. F. Emerging model systems for functional genomics analysis of crassulacean acid metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           31, 100–108 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2445">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dever, L. V., Boxall, S. F., Kneřová, J. &amp; Hartwell, J. Transgenic perturbation of the decarboxylation phase of crassulacean acid metabolism alters physiology and metabolism but has only a small effect on growth. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           167, 44–59 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2448">40</a></sup>, whereas in pineapple a combination of MDH, working in the OAA-forming direction, coupled with PEPCK, converting OAA to PEP and CO<sub>2</sub>, are the candidate decarboxylation enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2455">18</a></sup>, consistent with previous enzyme activity studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Christopher, J. &amp; Holtum, J. Patterns of carbohydrate partitioning in the leaves of crassulacean acid metabolism species during deacidification. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           112, 393–399 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2459">8</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Convergent evolution of genes involved in stomatal movement</h3><p>A unique feature of CAM physiology is the inverted light/dark pattern of stomatal movement relative to C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis, with stomata opening during the night in CAM and during the day in C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2475">6</a></sup>. Blue light is a key environmental signal that controls stomatal opening and phototropin 2 (PHOT2; AT5G58140), a blue light photoreceptor, mediates blue light regulation of stomatal opening in <i>Arabidopsis</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kinoshita, T. et al. Phot1 and phot2 mediate blue light regulation of stomatal opening. Nature &#xA;                           414, 656–660 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2482">42</a></sup>. Twenty genes that could potentially be involved in stomatal movement in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> were predicted based on homology to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes involved in the regulation of stomatal movement (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">11</a>). One of these genes, Kaladp0033s0113, which encodes PHOT2, showed only a 1-h time shift in transcript abundance pattern relative to its <i>A. comosus</i> ortholog (Aco014242) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>), possibly indicating a convergent change in the diel pattern of its transcript abundance pattern in the two CAM species. In support of a convergent evolution hypothesis, the transcript abundance patterns of the two <i>PHOT2</i> genes in the CAM species showed 11- (<i>Kalanchoë</i>) and 9- (pineapple) hour phase shifts, respectively, relative to that of the <i>PHOT2</i> gene (AT5G58140) in the C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4c</a>). The timing of peak transcript abundance shifted from dawn in <i>Arabidopsis</i> to dusk in the two CAM species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4c</a>). This convergent change in diel transcript abundance pattern suggests that PHOT2 might contribute to the inverted day/night pattern of stomatal closure and opening in CAM species such that PHOT2 might function as a switch mediating the blue-light signal to open the stomata at dusk and the stomata could then remain open during the dark period.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Convergent evolution of genes involved in heat tolerance</h3><p>The stomata of mature CAM leaves of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> close for the majority of the light period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dever, L. V., Boxall, S. F., Kneřová, J. &amp; Hartwell, J. Transgenic perturbation of the decarboxylation phase of crassulacean acid metabolism alters physiology and metabolism but has only a small effect on growth. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           167, 44–59 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2538">40</a></sup>, which may exacerbate the internal heat load on the leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Krause, G. H., Winter, K., Krause, B. &amp; Virgo, A. Protection by light against heat stress in leaves of tropical crassulacean acid metabolism plants containing high acid levels. Funct. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           43, 1061–1069 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2542">43</a></sup>. Photosynthesis is sensitive to heat stress and can be inhibited long before other symptoms of heat stress are detected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Berry, J. &amp; Björkman, O. Photosynthetic response and adaptation to temperature in higher plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           31, 491–543 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2546">44</a></sup>. Numerous studies have shown that the inhibition of photosynthesis by moderate heat stress is a consequence of RuBisCO deactivation, caused, in part, by the thermal instability of RuBisCO activase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salvucci, M. E. &amp; Crafts-Brandner, S. J. Mechanism for deactivation of Rubisco under moderate heat stress. Physiol. Plant. &#xA;                           122, 513–519 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2550">45</a></sup>. Heat-shock proteins can play a critical role in the stabilization of proteins under heat stress conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, W., Vinocur, B., Shoseyov, O. &amp; Altman, A. Role of plant heat-shock proteins and molecular chaperones in the abiotic stress response. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           9, 244–252 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2555">46</a></sup>. Wang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wang, G. et al. A tomato chloroplast-targeted DnaJ protein protects Rubisco activity under heat stress. J. Exp. Bot. &#xA;                           66, 3027–3240 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2559">47</a></sup> reported that HSP40 (SlCDJ2) contributed to the maintenance of CO<sub>2</sub> assimilation capacity mainly by protecting RuBisCO activity under heat stress and that HSP70 (cpHsp70) acted as a binding partner for SlCDJ2 in tomato. HSP70 can also function as nano-compartments in which single RbcL/RbcS subunits can fold in isolation, unimpaired by aggregation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Liu, C. et al. Coupled chaperone action in folding and assembly of hexadecameric Rubisco. Nature &#xA;                           463, 197–202 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2565">48</a></sup>, as illustrated in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig8">8a</a>. Among the HSP70 genes predicted in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, Kaladp0060s0296 displayed peak transcript abundance in the morning, with only a 1-h shift in diel transcript abundance pattern relative to its <i>A. comosus</i> ortholog Aco031458, whereas these two HSP70 genes in the CAM species showed ~10-h shifts in diel transcript abundance pattern relative to their best-matched <i>A. thaliana</i> ortholog, AT5G02490 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig8">8b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>), suggesting that HSP70 has undergone convergent changes in diel transcript expression patterns during the evolution of CAM.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Convergent changes in diel transcript expression of heat-shock proteins (HSPs) in CAM species in comparison with C<sub>3</sub> species. <b>a</b> Schematic representation of the possible roles of HSP40, HSP60, and HSP70 in leaf heat tolerance. <b>b</b> Comparison of diel transcript expression pattern of HSP70 between CAM species (<i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i> and pineapple) and C<sub>3</sub> species (<i>Arabidopsis</i>). Left panel shows the diel transcript expression patterns. Right panel shows enrichment triangle network, in which a <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> gene and a pineapple ortholog had significantly enriched expression in the same time-window, whereas an <i>Arabidopsis</i> ortholog had significantly enriched expression in the opposite time-window. The numbers are the time shifts in diel transcript expression pattern between genes connected by each edge. White and black bars indicate daytime (12-h) and nighttime (12-h), respectively. <i>X</i>-axis represents the time after the beginning of the light period. RuBisCO: Ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase; RCA: rubisco activase; RuBP: ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate; PGA: 3-phosphoglycerate</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Convergent evolution of genes in the circadian clock</h3><p>Key physiological and biochemical features of CAM including net CO<sub>2</sub> exchange and PEPC phosphorylation are well established as outputs of the circadian clock, displaying robust oscillation under free-running constant conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hartwell, J., Dever, L. V. &amp; Boxall, S. F. Emerging model systems for functional genomics analysis of crassulacean acid metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           31, 100–108 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2643">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dever, L. V., Boxall, S. F., Kneřová, J. &amp; Hartwell, J. Transgenic perturbation of the decarboxylation phase of crassulacean acid metabolism alters physiology and metabolism but has only a small effect on growth. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           167, 44–59 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2646">40</a></sup>. Thus, the circadian clock could be a key regulator of the diel reprogramming of metabolism and stomatal function that defines CAM. The molecular basis of circadian rhythms has been studied extensively in non-CAM species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hsu, P. Y. &amp; Harmer, S. L. Wheels within wheels: the plant circadian system. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 240–249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2650">33</a></sup>. Based on homology to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes that have been shown to play important roles as molecular components of the circadian clock, 35 <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes were predicted to be involved in circadian rhythms (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>). None of these <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes are among the list of genes showing convergent changes in diel expression pattern (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a>), suggesting that CAM evolution did not involve major changes in the diel expression pattern of these known circadian rhythm genes shared between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. However, we cannot rule out the possibility of convergent evolution in unknown circadian rhythm genes between these two species. Also, it is possible that genes that are not involved in circadian rhythms in <i>Arabidopsis</i> could have taken on this function in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. On the other hand, Kaladp0060s0460, which encodes ELONGATED HYPOCOTYL5 (HY5), showed a convergent change in protein sequences between <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a>). HY5 is a bZIP family transcription factor in the blue light signaling pathway that acts as an input to entrain the circadian clock<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hsu, P. Y. &amp; Harmer, S. L. Wheels within wheels: the plant circadian system. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 240–249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2692">33</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig9">9a</a>). A single amino-acid mutation (E-to-R) occurred in the C-terminal bZIP domains of the proteins encoded by Kaladp0060s0460 and its <i>P. equestris</i> ortholog PEQU_13446 as compared with HY5 from C<sub>3</sub> or C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig9">9b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">14</a>). The bZIP domain determines the DNA-binding ability of HY5 as a transcription factor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Nijhawan, A., Jain, M., Tyagi, A. K. &amp; Khurana, J. P. Genomic survey and gene expression analysis of the basic leucine zipper transcription factor family in rice. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           146, 333–350 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2713">49</a></sup>, mediating the interaction between HY5 and G-BOX BINDING FACTOR 1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ram, H. &amp; Chattopadhyay, S. Molecular interaction of bZIP domains of GBF1, HY5 and HYH in Arabidopsis seedling development. Plant Signal. Behav. &#xA;                           8, e22703 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2718">50</a></sup>. HY5 has been shown to move from shoot to root to coordinate aboveground plant carbon uptake in the leaf and belowground nitrogen acquisition in the root<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Chen, X. et al. Shoot-to-root mobile transcription factor HY5 coordinates plant carbon and nitrogen acquisition. Curr. Biol. &#xA;                           26, 640–646 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2722">51</a></sup>. Therefore, the potential roles of HY5, Kaladp0060s0460, in circadian rhythmicity and shoot-to-root communication in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> needs to be investigated using experimental approaches such as loss-of-function mutagenesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, D., Hu, R., Palla, K. J., Tuskan, G. A. &amp; Yang, X. Advances and perspectives on the use of CRISPR/Cas9 systems in plant genomics research. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           30, 70–77 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2729">52</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig9_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01491-7/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1491_Fig9_HTML.jpg" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>A convergent change in elongated hypocotyl 5 (HY5) protein sequences in CAM species. <b>a</b> An overview of the signaling pathway involved in circadian rhythm in plants. <b>b</b> Convergent change in HY5 protein sequences in diverse species (highlighted in red font). The black line indicates the protein sequence alignment position (located within the bZIP domain) where the mutation (E-to-R) occurred. CCA1 circadian clock associated 1, COP1 constitutive photomorphogenic 1, CRY cryptochrome, EC evening complex, ELF 3/4 early flowering 3/4, GI gigantea, LHY late elongated hypocotyl, LUX lux arrhythmo, PRR5/7/9 pinoresinol reductase 5/7/9, PHYs phytochromes, RVEs reveilles, TOC1 timing of cab expression 1</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Convergent evolution of genes in carbohydrate metabolism</h3><p>Nocturnal production of phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) as a substrate for dark CO<sub>2</sub> uptake represents a substantial sink for carbohydrates in CAM plants, which has to be balanced with the provision of carbohydrates for growth and maintenance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Borland, A. M., Guo, H.-B., Yang, X. &amp; Cushman, J. C. Orchestration of carbohydrate processing for crassulacean acid metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           31, 118–124 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2768">53</a></sup>. Carbohydrate active enzymes (CAZymes) play critical roles in regulating carbohydrate synthesis, metabolism, and transport in living organisms. There are six CAZyme classes: glycoside hydrolases (GHs), glycosyltransferases (GTs), polysaccharide lyases, carbohydrate esterases, auxiliary activities, and carbohydrate-binding modules. Each of these classes contains from a dozen to over one hundred different protein families based on sequence similarity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lombard, V., Golaconda Ramulu, H., Drula, E., Coutinho, P. M. &amp; Henrissat, B. The carbohydrate-active enzymes database (CAZy) in 2013. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           42, D490–D495 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2772">54</a></sup>. The six classes of CAZymes have different functions. For example, GH enzymes catalyze the hydrolysis of glycosidic bonds, while GT enzymes catalyze the formation of glycosidic bonds. Using CAZyme domain-specific hidden Markov models, defined in the dbCAN database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yin, Y. et al. dbCAN: a web resource for automated carbohydrate-active enzyme annotation. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, W445–W451 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2776">55</a></sup>, we identified 100 CAZyme families, including 1093 genes in the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome, comparable to the total number (1149) of CAZyme genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM7">5</a>). Among these CAZyme genes, four ortholog groups (ORTHOMCL68, ORTHOMCL93, ORTHOMCL207, and ORTHOMCL9830) of genes (e.g., Kaladp0550s0020, Kaladp0011s0363, Kaladp0037s0421, Kaladp0055s0317, respectively) belonging to the CAZyme families GH100, GT20, GT2, and GT5, respectively, displayed convergent changes in their patterns of diel transcript abundance in two CAM species (<i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>A. comosus</i>) compared with the C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species (<i>A. thaliana</i>) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a>). Specifically, the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> CAZyme genes with convergent changes in diel transcript abundance pattern (e.g., Kaladp0550s0020 [GH100], Kaladp0011s0363 [GH20], Kaladp0037s0421 [GT2], and Kaladp0055s0317 [GT5]) showed higher transcript abundance in the dark and early light period (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">19</a>). In particular, two genes (Kaladp0011s0363 and Kaladp0055s0317) were predicted to be involved in starch and sucrose metabolism (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">20</a>). Kaladp0011s0363 encodes a probable trehalose phosphate synthase. Trehalose 6-phosphate is an important sugar signaling metabolite and is thought to link starch degradation to demand for sucrose and growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Martins, M. C. M. et al. Feedback inhibition of starch degradation in Arabidopsis leaves mediated by trehalose 6-phosphate. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           163, 1142–1163 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2817">56</a></sup>. Kaladp0550s0020 encodes an alkaline-neutral invertase that catalyzes the hydrolysis of sucrose to glucose and fructose. This invertase has also been implicated in metabolic signaling processes as an important regulator of plant growth and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Xiang, L. et al. Exploring the neutral invertase–oxidative stress defence connection in Arabidopsis thaliana. J. Exp. Bot. &#xA;                           62, 3849–3862 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2822">57</a></sup>. Taken together, these data suggest that the evolution of CAM from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis requires re-scheduling of the transcription of metabolic and signaling genes that regulate the partitioning of carbohydrates between reserves that provide substrates for CAM and carbohydrates required for growth.</p><p>In addition to the above convergent changes in expression pattern of four CAZyme genes, we also identified convergent changes in protein sequences of another two CAZyme genes (Kaladp0016s0058 [GT29] and Kaladp0067s0114 [GH35]) that were under positive selection (CodeML implemented in PosiGene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Sahm A., Bens M., Platzer M., Szafranski K. PosiGene: automated and easy-to-use pipeline for genome-wide detection of positively selected genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, e100, (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2831">58</a></sup>, <i>P &lt; </i>0.05) in the dicot CAM species <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and one of the two monocot CAM species (<i>A. comosus</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>) (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>). Kaladp0016s0058 encodes a putative sialyltransferase-like protein. Two single amino-acid mutations were found in Kaladp0016s0058 and its <i>A. comosus</i> ortholog Aco018360, as compared with the orthologous protein sequences of non-CAM species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>). These two mutations are close to each other (i.e., within a four-amino-acid distance), suggesting the possibility that the two mutations affect the same functional domain. Kaladp0067s0114 encodes a beta-galactosidase protein that hydrolyses the glycosidic bond between two or more carbohydrates. Two single amino-acid mutations were identified in Kaladp0067s0114 and its <i>P. equestris</i> ortholog PEQU_04899, as compared with the orthologous protein sequences of non-CAM species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>). These two mutations are close to each other (i.e., within an 11-amino-acid distance) in the middle of galactose-binding domain (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>), which can bind to specific ligands and carbohydrate substrates for enzymatic catalytic reactions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ito, N. &amp; Phillips, S. E. Novel thioether bond revealed by a 1.7 A crystal structure of galactose oxidase. Nature &#xA;                           350, 87 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2870">59</a></sup>. The relevance of these convergent changes in protein sequence to CAM evolution needs further investigation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>The CAM pathway has been found in 36 families of vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2883">4</a></sup>, among which Crassulaceae plays a unique role in CAM research because the pathway was first discovered in this succulent plant family and was thus named<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Black, C. C. &amp; Osmond, C. B. Crassulacean acid metabolism photosynthesis: ‘working the night shift’. Photosynth. Res. &#xA;                           76, 329–341 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2887">60</a></sup>. Within Crassulaceae, the genus <i>Kalanchoë</i> has been the most widely used for CAM research. As a model species for research into the molecular biology and functional genomics of CAM, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> stands out due to its relatively small genome, low repetitive sequence content, and efficient stable transformation protocols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hartwell, J., Dever, L. V. &amp; Boxall, S. F. Emerging model systems for functional genomics analysis of crassulacean acid metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           31, 100–108 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2897">20</a></sup>. The genome sequence presented in this study renders <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> as a new model for plant evolutionary and comparative genomics research, both for CAM photosynthesis and beyond. Although this study focused on genome-wide analysis of convergent evolution in CAM plants, the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome data can be used to facilitate CAM research related to: (1) generating loss-of-function mutants for functional characterization of CAM-related genes using genome-editing technology; (2) deciphering the regulation of CAM genes through identification of transcription factors and promoters of their target genes; (3) analyzing CAM gene expression by serving as a template for mapping of RNA sequencing reads and protein mass spectrometry data; and (4) identifying DNA polymorphisms related to genetic diversity of plants in the genus <i>Kalanchoë</i>.</p><p>Our genome-wide comparison of CAM species and non-CAM species revealed two types of convergent changes that could be informative with respect to the evolution of CAM: protein sequence convergence and convergent changes in the diel re-scheduling of transcript abundance. In the present study, a total of 60 genes exhibited convergent evolution in divergent eudicot and monocot CAM lineages. Specifically, we identified protein sequence convergence in six genes involved in nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation, circadian rhythm, carbohydrate metabolism, and so on (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>). Also, we identified convergent diel expression changes in 54 genes that are involved in stomatal movement, heat stress response, carbohydrate metabolism, and so on (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a>). These results provide strong support for our hypothesis that convergent evolution in protein sequence or gene temporal expression underpins the multiple and independent emergences of CAM from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis. New systems biology tools and genome-editing technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, D., Hu, R., Palla, K. J., Tuskan, G. A. &amp; Yang, X. Advances and perspectives on the use of CRISPR/Cas9 systems in plant genomics research. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           30, 70–77 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2931">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="De Paoli, H. C., Tuskan, G. A. &amp; Yang, X. H. An innovative platform for quick and flexible joining of assorted DNA fragments. Sci. Rep. &#xA;                           6, 19278 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2934">61</a></sup> offer great potential for plant functional genomics research based on loss- or gain-of-function mutants to characterize the role of the genes predicted here to have undergone convergent evolution.</p><p>Convergent gene function can arise by (1) a mutation or mutations in the same gene or genes that result in homoplasy in organisms or (2) independent causal mutation or mutations in different genes in each lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Washburn, J. D., Bird, K. A., Conant, G. C., Pires, J. C. &amp; Herendeen, P. S. Convergent evolution and the origin of complex phenotypes in the age of systems biology. Int. J. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           177, 305–318 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2941">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Wake, D. B., Wake, M. H. &amp; Specht, C. D. Homoplasy: from detecting pattern to determining process and mechanism of evolution. Science &#xA;                           331, 1032–1035 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d49345e2944">62</a></sup>. We identified four genes that showed convergent changes in protein sequences, none of which were shared by the three CAM species <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, and <i>P. equestris</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">15</a>), suggesting that CAM convergences result mainly from the second scenario. Alternatively, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> shares the convergent mutation in the PEPC2 protein sequence with <i>P. equestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig6">6</a>), whereas it shares the convergent change in the pattern of diel transcript abundance of <i>PPCK1</i> with <i>A. comosus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#Fig4">4b</a>). These results suggest that two alternative modes of convergent evolution could have occurred in pathways for nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation. First, PPCK shifted from light period to dark period to promote the activation of PEPC1 (the most abundant isoform), as exemplified by <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>A. comosus</i>. Second, a single amino-acid mutation from R/K/H to D to maintain the active state of PEPC2, without the need for phosphorylation, then occurred, as in <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>.</p><p>According to the constrained selection theory of Morris<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Morris S. C. Life’s Solution: Inevitable Humans In A Lonely Universe. Cambridge University (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3004">11</a></sup>, we expected to see convergent changes in protein sequences in all the three CAM species. However, in this study, single-site mutations were found in only two of the three CAM species. Our additional positive selection analysis revealed that <i>Kalanchoë</i> did share convergent sequence mutation with the other two CAM species, but at alternate sites (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>). This is consistent with a recent report showing that single amino-acid mutations were not shared by all the bird species that displayed convergent evolution of hemoglobin function as an adaptation to high-altitude environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Natarajan, C. et al. Predictable convergence in hemoglobin function has unpredictable molecular underpinnings. Science &#xA;                           354, 336–339 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3017">14</a></sup>. Alternatively, our results, to some extent, support the contingent adaptation theory of Gould<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gould S. J. Wonderful Life: The Burgess Shale And The Nature Of Life. Norton (1989)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3022">15</a></sup>. The relevance of these predicted convergent changes to CAM needs to be investigated using experimental approaches, such as transferring the convergent CAM genes to C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species to test the effect of these genes on C<sub>3</sub>-to-CAM photosynthesis transition.</p><p>In this study, we did not identify any gene that exhibited both convergent changes in transcript abundance patterns (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a>) and convergent changes in protein sequence (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>), suggesting that convergent evolution of a gene in CAM species was achieved through either protein sequence convergence or rewiring of gene expression. Indeed, we have not seen any reports showing that both protein convergence and convergent gene expression change occurred in the same gene in any type of organisms to date. Thus, we can hypothesize that convergent evolution follows the “law of parsimony” that emphasizes the fewest possible assumptions for explaining a thing or event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Laird, J. The law of parsimony. The Monist &#xA;                           29, 321–344 (1919)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3046">63</a></sup>. An implication of this hypothesis is that reuse of the key genes via altered diel expression patterns would be the shortest path for C<sub>3</sub>-to-CAM photosynthesis evolution; and on the other hand, mutations in some key sites of protein sequences, while keeping the temporal gene expression pattern unchanged, would be the shortest path for evolving new protein function required by CAM. Although our data fit this hypothesis, additional screens for genes that have convergent changes in both protein sequence and expression pattern in the future are merited.</p><p>Increasing human population and changes in global temperature and precipitation are creating major challenges for the sustainable supply of food, fiber, and fuel in the years to come. As a proven mechanism for increasing WUE in plants, CAM offers great potential for meeting these challenges. Engineering of CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis plants could be a viable strategy to improve WUE in non-CAM crops for food and biomass production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3058">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3061">6</a></sup>. The genes predicted here to have undergone convergent evolution during the emergence of CAM are crucial candidates for CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis engineering. Our results suggest that CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis engineering requires rewiring of the diel transcript abundance patterns for most of the candidate genes in the target C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species, along with amino-acid mutations in the protein sequences of several other candidate genes. Specifically, CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis engineering efforts should be focused on changing the temporal patterns of transcript expression of endogenous genes in the target C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species corresponding to the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes listed in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM5">3</a>. CRISPR/Cas9-based knock-in approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, D., Hu, R., Palla, K. J., Tuskan, G. A. &amp; Yang, X. Advances and perspectives on the use of CRISPR/Cas9 systems in plant genomics research. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           30, 70–77 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3082">52</a></sup> can be used to replace the original endogenous promoters of the target genes with temporal promoters that confer temporal expression patterns similar to those of their orthologous genes in the CAM species. For example, dark-inducible promoters such as Din10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Fujiki, Y. et al. Dark‐inducible genes from Arabidopsis thaliana are associated with leaf senescence and repressed by sugars. Physiol. Plant. &#xA;                           111, 345–352 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3086">64</a></sup> can be used to drive the expression of carboxylation gene modules during the nighttime and light-inducible promoters, such as GT1-GATA-NOS101<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Puente, P., Wei, N. &amp; Deng, X. W. Combinatorial interplay of promoter elements constitutes the minimal determinants for light and developmental control of gene expression in Arabidopsis. EMBO J. &#xA;                           15, 3732 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3091">65</a></sup>, can be used to drive the expression of decarboxylation gene modules during the daytime. To make the protein sequence changes needed for CAM-into-C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis engineering, transferring the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genes listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a> to target C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis species via the <i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated transformation could provide a relatively straightforward path to an efficient engineered CAM pathway. Alternatively, one could mutate the amino acids shown in Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">15</a> using a knock-in strategy with emerging genome-editing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, D., Hu, R., Palla, K. J., Tuskan, G. A. &amp; Yang, X. Advances and perspectives on the use of CRISPR/Cas9 systems in plant genomics research. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. &#xA;                           30, 70–77 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3115">52</a></sup>.</p><p>In summary, this study provides an important model genome for studying plant comparative, functional, and evolutionary genomics, as well as significant advances in our understanding of CAM evolution. Our findings hold tremendous potential to accelerate the genetic improvement of crops for enhanced drought avoidance and sustainable production of food and bioenergy on marginal lands.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant material</h3><p>
                           <i>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</i> ‘M2’ plants were purchased from Mass Spectrum Botanicals (Tampa, FL, USA) (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Estimation of DNA content</h3><p>The DNA contents of young leaf tissue samples were analyzed using flow cytometry analysis service provided by Plant Cytometry Services (The Netherlands). The internal standard was <i>Vinca minor</i> (DNA = 1.51 pg/2 C = 1477 Mbp/2 C).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Chromosome counting</h3><p>Images were collected using an Olympus FluoView FV1000 confocal microscope (Center Valley, PA, USA) with a 60× objective. Images were sharpened using Adobe Photoshop and chromosomes were counted using ImageJ software (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Illumina sequencing of genome</h3><p>The genomic DNA libraries of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> were sequenced on a MiSeq instrument (Illumina, CA, USA) using MiSeq Reagent Kit v3 (600-cycle) (Illumina, CA, USA) (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>In order to capture mRNA abundance changes responsive to diel conditions, samples were collected in triplicate from mature <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> leaves (i.e., the fifth and sixth mature leaf pairs counting from the top) every 2 h over a 24 h time course under 12 h light/12 h dark photoperiod. Additional tissues were sampled in triplicate including roots, flowers, shoot tips plus young leaves, and stems at one time point, 4 h after the beginning of the light period (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Genome assembly and improvement</h3><p>The <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome was initially assembled using platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. &#xA;                           24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3202">66</a></sup> from 70X Illumina paired-end reads (2 × 300 bp reads; unamplified 540 bp whole-genome shotgun fragment library), and three mate-libraries (3 kb, 14X; 6 kb, 12X; 11 kb, 11X). Further genome scaffolding was performed using MeDuSa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Bosi, E. et al. MeDuSa: a multi-draft based scaffolder. Bioinformatics 31, (2443–2451 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3206">67</a></sup> sequentially with the genome assemblies of <i>K. laxiflora</i> v1.1 (Phytozome), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> Genoscope.12X (Phytozome), and <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> v3.4 (Phytozome).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Protein-coding gene annotation</h3><p>The genome annotation for <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> was performed using homology-based predictors facilitated with transcript assemblies (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Construction of orthologous groups</h3><p>The protein sequences of 26 plant species were selected for ortholog group construction (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Construction of species phylogeny</h3><p>The phylogeny of plant species was constructed from the protein sequences of 210 single-copy genes identified through analysis of orthologous groups (see “Construction of orthologous groups” section). The details for species phylogeny construction are described in Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">8</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Construction of protein tribes and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The protein sequences used for ortholog analysis (see “Construction of orthologous groups”) were also clustered into tribes using TRIBE-MCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Enright, A. J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C. A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           30, 1575–1584 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3264">68</a></sup>, with a BLASTp E-value cutoff of 1e-5 and an inflation value of 5.0. Phylogenetic analysis of the protein tribes is described in Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">9</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Analysis of convergence in protein sequences in CAM species</h3><p>The phylogenetic trees of protein tribes (see aforementioned “Construction of protein tribes and phylogenetic analysis”) were examined to identify the “CAM-convergence” clade, which was defined to contain genes from <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> (dicot) and at least one of the two monocot CAM species (<i>A. comosus</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>) without any genes from C<sub>3</sub> or C<sub>4</sub> species. The rationale for defining the “CAM-convergence” clade is that the dicot CAM species <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> should be separated from the monocot CAM species if there is no convergence between <i>Kalanchoë</i> and the monocot CAM species (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Gene Ontology analysis and pathway annotation</h3><p>Whole-genome gene ontology (GO) term annotation was performed using BLAST2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Gotz, S. et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           36, 3420–3435 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3311">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3314">70</a></sup> with a BLASTP E-value hit filter of 1 × 10<sup>−6</sup>, an annotation cutoff value of 55, and GO weight of 5. The enrichment of GO biological process and pathway annotation are described in Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">11</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">Analysis of carbohydrate active enzymes</h3><p>The protein sequences were searched against the dbCAN database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yin, Y. et al. dbCAN: a web resource for automated carbohydrate-active enzyme annotation. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, W445–W451 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3331">55</a></sup> using HMMER3 (<a href="http://hmmer.org/">http://hmmer.org/</a>). The HMMER search outputs were parsed to keep significant hits with E-value &lt;1e-23 (calculated by HMMER) and coverage &gt;0.2 (calculated on the HMM, which is equal to (end position - start position)/total length of HMM), as suggested by a large scale benchmark analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ekstrom, A., Taujale, R., McGinn, N. &amp; Yin, Y. PlantCAZyme: a database for plant carbohydrate-active enzymes. Database (Oxford) &#xA;                           2014, bau079 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3342">71</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec28">Estimation of transcript abundance in <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                        </h3><p>The RNA-seq data in fastq format were mapped to the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome using TopHat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           14, 1 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3361">72</a></sup>. Transcript abundance in FPKM (Fragments Per Kilobase of transcript per Million mapped reads) was estimated using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. &#xA;                           7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3365">73</a></sup>. All mapped read counts of the transcripts were counted by using htseq-count, a subprogram of HTseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq—a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3369">74</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec29">Co-expression network analysis in <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                        </h3><p>The expression data of 16 samples in triplicates were used for co-expression network analysis, which included time-course data (12 time points: 2, 4, 6, …, 24 h after the beginning of the light period) from mature leaf and one time point data (4 h after the beginning of the light period) from roots, flowers, stems, and shoot tips plus young leaves collected in triplicate from the <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> plants grown under 12 h light/12 h dark photoperiod. The details for co-expression network analysis are described in Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">12</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec30">Cluster analysis of gene expression in <i>Kalanchoë</i>
                        </h3><p>Count values for each RNA-seq library were used to calculate polynomial regressions across time (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec31">Comparative analysis of gene expression</h3><p>The diurnal expression data with 4-h intervals for <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were obtained from Mockler et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Mockler, T. C. et al. The Diurnal project: diurnal and circadian expression profiling, model-based pattern matching, and promoter analysis. Cold Spring Harb. Symp. Quant. Biol. &#xA;                           72, 353–363 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3418">75</a></sup> and adjusted to 2-h interval time series by interpolation using the SRS1 cubic spline function (<a href="http://www.srs1software.com/">http://www.srs1software.com/</a>). The diurnal expression data with 2-h intervals for <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> was generated in this study. The diurnal expression data with 2-h intervals for <i>Ananas comosus</i> was obtained from Ming et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yang, X. et al. A roadmap for research on crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) to enhance sustainable food and bioenergy production in a hotter, drier world. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 491–504 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3436">4</a></sup>. The gene expression data were normalized by Z-score transformation. The hierarchical clustering of gene expression was performed for genes in each ortholog group using the Bioinformatics Toolbox in Matlab (Mathworks, Inc.) based on Spearman correlation (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec32">Genome synteny analysis</h3><p>Pairwise genome alignments were performed between grape genome (Genoscope.12X; <a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>) and <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec33">Protein 3D structural simulation</h3><p>The protein structural models were built using the iterative threading assembly refinement (I-TASSER, V4.3) structural modeling toolkit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Roy, A., Kucukural, A. &amp; Zhang, Y. I-TASSER: a unified platform for automated protein structure and function prediction. Nat. Protoc. &#xA;                           5, 725–738 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3472">76</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Yang, J. et al. The I-TASSER Suite: protein structure and function prediction. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           12, 7–8 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d49345e3475">77</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec34">Gas chromatography-mass spectrometry metabolite profiling</h3><p>For the major metabolites of <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, a total of 36 leaf samples (the 5th and 6th fully expanded leaf pairs counting from the top) were collected with three biological replicates sampled every 2-h for a 24-h diurnal cycle. Additionally, three biological replicate samples of stems, roots, shoot tips plus young leaves, and flowers were also collected (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec35">In vitro protein expression and analysis of enzyme activity</h3><p>The PEPC proteins were expressed in bacterial BL21strains (Novagen BL21 (DE3) pLysS Singles), and purified via Glutathione Sepharose 4B beads (GE Healthcare Life Sciences, Pittsburgh, PA, USA). The protein quality was checked via western blot using anti-PEPC antibody (Agrisera, Sweden) and the PEPC activity was determined (Supplementary Method <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01491-7#MOESM2">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec36">Data availability</h3><p>The Department of Energy (DOE) will provide public access to these results of federally sponsored research in accordance with the DOE Public Access Plan (<a href="http://energy.gov/downloads/doe-public-access-plan">http://energy.gov/downloads/doe-public-access-plan</a>). The <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome sequence and annotation are deposited in Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>). The <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> genome sequence is also deposited at NCBI GenBank (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genbank/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genbank/</a>) under the accession code NQLW00000000. The genome sequencing reads are deposited in NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>) with the BioSample accession SAMN07509503, which is the combination of the five individual BioSamples: SAMN07453935, SAMN07453936, SAMN07453937, SAMN07453938, and SAMN07453939. The RNA-Seq reads are deposited in NCBI SRA with the BioSample accession codes SAMN07453940 - SAMN07453987. The metabolite data is deposited at MetaboLights (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/metabolights/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/metabolights/</a>) under the accession code MTBLS519.</p></div></div></section>

W., R.V.B., and R.M. contributed pineapple genomics and gene expression data; D.A.W., R.C.M., and D.A.J. carried out convergent expression analysis; P.E.A. and R.L.H. carried out GO and metabolite data analysis; K.W. interpreted the data; J.A.C.S. interpreted the data and wrote the manuscript; E.F., R.M., H.C.D.P., A.M.B., and Y.Y. carried out metabolic pathway analysis; Z.Z. and T.J.T. carried out metabolite profiling; J.D.B. carried out phylogenetic analysis; H.-B.G. and H.G. carried out phylogenetic analysis and protein structure modeling; K.H. and J.H.L-M. carried out phylogenetic analysis, protein structure clustering, and wrote the manuscript; J.M.H. and K.J.P. carried out ploidy analysis; M.X., J-G.C. and W.M. contributed to protein function characterization; W.C.Y. carried out RNA-seq data analysis and interpreted the data; D.L. carried out stomatal and circadian gene analysis; R.A., T.G., J.A.M., and S-D.L. contributed to transcriptome and genome sequencing; J.G. contributed to transcriptome sequencing; J.C.C. contributed to transcriptome, genome sequencing, and wrote the manuscript; G.A.T. conceived the study and interpreted the data. All authors read and commented on manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
